PREFACE. 


The  situation  of  Newbattle  Is  of  that  kind  wliich  the  Cistercians  most 
of  all  affected.  The  South  Esk,  escaped  from  the  green  hUls  of  Temple 
and  the  woody  ravines  of  Dalhousie,  widens  its  valley  a  httle  to  give 
room  for  a  long  range  of  fair  level  '  haughs.'  At  the  very  head  of 
tliese  meadows,  and  close  to  the  brook,  the  Abbey  stands.  Behind,  to 
thi!  north,  are  the  remains  of  the  ancient  monkish  village,  onee  occupied 
hy  the  hinds  and  shepherds  of  tlie  convent,  but  separated  from  the  Abbey 
gardens  by  a  massive  stonc  wall,  ascribed  to  the  time  and  the  personal  care 
of  Wilham  the  Lion,'  which  still  forms  the  boundary  of  the  park  on  that 
side.  Across  the  Uttle  river  the  bank  rlses  abruptly,  broken  into  fantastlc 
ravlnes,  closely  Avooded,  which  only  upon  examinatlon  are  discovered  to 
be  the  reniains  of  the  ancient  coal-worklngs  uf  the  monks,  of  a  pcriod  when 
the  operatlon  was  more  a  sort  of  quarrylng  than  hke  modern  coal-mlning. 
The  Abbey  was  not  placed  to  command  a  prospect.  The  rlver  banks 
have  probably  always  been  covered  with  a  growth  of  native  oak.  What 
was  the  clothing  of  the  level  lawn  of  old  we  can  only  conjecture.     As  it  Is,

The  next  abbot  was  Roger,  previously  the  cellarer  of  the  Abbey  of 
Mebose.  It  is  noted  that,  in  the  incumbency  of  tliis  abbot,  Robert 
de  Keldelach,  Abbot  of  DunfermUn,  and  Chancellor  of  Scotland,  being 
obliged  to  rehnquish  his  office  and  abbacy  for  his  share  in  the  Duvward 
plot,  retired  as  a  monk  into  the  Abbey  of  Neubotle  in  1252,  and  there 
continued  tiU  1269,  when  he  was  chosen  Abbot  of  Mchros,  and  ruled  that 
great  monastery  till  his  death,  wldch  took  place  in  1273,  '  non  sine  magna 
sanctitatis  opinionc.'  Abbot  Roger  of  Neubotle  as&isted  at  the  conference 
between  Henry  III.  of  England  and  the  young  king  Alexander  III.  of 
Scotland,  at  Roxburgh,  on  the  20th  September  1255.  He  attended  a 
chapter  of  his  order  held  in  England,  and,  on  his  return  towards  Scotland. 
died  at  the  monastery  of  Vaudey  in  1256.' 

Welliam,  the  prior,  was  chosen  abbot  in  his  stead.  He  became  bound  to 
pay  a  half  mark  yearly  to  the  nuns  of  North  Berwick,  as  the  rent  of  the 
land  in  Leith  and  Greenside,  the  okl  gift  of  the  lords  of  Restabic  to  the 
nunnery,  now  transferred  to  the  convent  of  Ncubotle.^  He,  with  consent 
of  his  chapter,  granted  to  Adam  Hunter  the  hereditary  ofRce  of  *  sergeantcy ' 
in  all  oauses  touching  life  and  hmb  throughout  the  abbey  land  of  Craufurd.^ 
WilUam  resigned  the  abbacy  in  1259.'' 

Adam,  ceUarer  of  Mekose,  succeeded,  being  inducted,  and  perhaps  con- 
secrated,  abbot  of  N^cubotle,  on  St  Yincenfs  day,  1 259.  He  was  unani-

the  Abbey  estates. 

Though  Neubotle  was  not  one  of  the  most  richly  endowed  monasteries 
of  Scotland,  the  Abbey  possessed  gi-eat  estates  in  six  counties,  Edinburgh, 
Haddiugton,  Linlithgow,  Lanark,  Peebles,  and  Stirling.  To  trace  these  pos- 
sessions  from  the  original  grants,  or  to  continue  their  liistory  fromthe 
Abbey  to  the  modern  holders,  however  interesting,  is  beyond  the  scope  of 
a  preface.  Scarcely  a  charter  of  this  volume  but  gives  us  early  infor- 
U)ation,  often  the  earhest,  of  the  district  to  which  it  refers;  scarcely  one 
wliich  docs  not  suggest  matters  very  interesting  to  the  local.  antiquary, 
affording  hght,  or  requiring  illustration.  But  sucli  pleasant  occupation 
must  be  left  to  the  topograplier  of  the  district,  who  caji,  by  the  help  of 
these  documents,  follow  an  ancient  boundary  through  the  changes  of  some 
centurles,  discover  the  strcngth  of  the  old  Korman  baron,  or  his  chapel 
of  forgotten  reverence,  among  com  fields  and  mountain  pastures,  and 
trace  the  settlement  and  civihzation  of  his  country  by  marks  that  cannot 
deceive,  though  they  uave  been  overlooked. 

A.  few  circumstances  connected  with  these  records  of  a  more  genera!  in- 
terest  are  all  that  can  be  here  adverted  to. 

The  monks  of  l^eubotle  wcre  probably  the  first  workers  of  ooal  in  ScotJand.

was  found  so  near  the  surfacc,  and  on  such  a  dechvity,  as  to  be  easily  wrought 
without  mining  or  expensive  operations  for  carrying  off  the  water.  Of 
the  period  when  that  bank  of  coal  was  worked  and  exhausted  we  find  no 
record.  But  the  charters  of  another  property  of  the  monastery  seem  to  throw 
light  upon  this  point.     The  first  charter  of  the  lands  of  Prestongrange  to

From  the  Lindesays,  also,  the  Abbey  received  extensive  grants  in  the  high 
range  of  Cra-vvfurd  at  an  early  period;  and  all  these  the  monks  turned  to 
good  account.  "We  have  seen  that  they  carried  on  mining  for  lead,  and  they 
did  so  also,  probably,  for  the  small  amount  of  the  precious  metals  which  that 
district  has  always  been  known  to  contain,  and  which  might  be  worth  the 
winning,  when  labour  and  subsistence  were  equally  cheap.  But  the  monks 
cultivated  their  Lanarkshire  territories  to  better  purpose  than  mining,  as 
it  was  then  practised.  They  kept  the  greater  part  in  their  oMvn  occupation. 
They  had  granges  at  each  of  their  '  towns  ' — each  grange  the  centre  of  a  oon- 
siderable  agricultural  estabhshment.  It  was  of  importance  to  preserve  an 
open  communication  with  those  distant  possessions,  and  the  Register  is  full 
of  transactions  for  that  object,  with  the  intermediate  proprietors,  whose 
grounds  must  be  passed  through.  The  grant  of  Alexander  II.,  of  Ucence 
to  pass  with  cattle  tlu-ough  any  intervening  ground,  and  to  spend  the  iiight 
in  the  common  pasture,  saving  corn  and  meadow,'  was  only  a  specifica- 
tion  of  an  ancient  common  law  right  in  Scotland.  But  the  monks,  being 
on  good  terms  with  theu-  neighbours,  accepted  the  right  sometimes  as  a 
grace.  Thus  the  knights  of  St  John  gave  free  passage  through  their  bounds 
of  Torphecliin ;  the  De  Boscos  lords  of  Ogilface,  through  their  land  of 
Ogilface;  the  Le  Chens  tlirough  Strabrock;  the  Stewarts  through  the 
barony  of  Bathcat ;  the  lords  of  Dalmahoy  through  their  territory.  The 
family  of  Melville  gave  a  very  early  hcence  of  the  same  kind  to  the  monks, 
'  flfoing  and  returning  between  Keubotle  and  the  Abbey  lands  in  Clydesdale, 
of  passLng  through  their  lands  of  Tletrevyn,  by  the  road  they  had  used  in 
times  past,  with  their  cattle  and  oarriages ;  and  also  of  unyokiug  their 
beasts  from  their  waggons,  and  pasturing  in  the  pasturage  of  that  land  as 
often  as  they  required,  avoiding  corn  and  meadow,  and  of  passing  the  night 
there,  once  in  going  and  once  in  returning.'  For  tliis  the  monks  were  to 
pay  yearly  a  new  waggon,  such  as  they  manufactured  for  their  own  use  in

The  western  possessions  of  I^eubotle  are  not  much  adapted  for  agricul- 
ture,  even  with  the  improved  management  and  probable  improvement  of 
chmate  of  niodern  times.  But  it  was  well  suited  for  rearing  stock,  and 
especially  for  wool-growing ;  and  we  have  some  curious  evidence  that  the 
Abbey  of  i^eubotle  took  a  lead  in  producing  the  finest  quahty  of  wool 
grown  in  Scotland.  A  ledger  has  been  preserved,  by  a  fortunate  accident, 
showing  the  transactions  of  Andrew  Hahburton,  a  merchant  at  Middle- 
burgh,  and  conservator  of  the  Scotch  privileges  in  the  Low  countries,  at  the 
end  of  the  fifteenth  centru-y.  His  chief  deahngs  were  with  his  countrymen; 
and  while  he  received  lasts  of  salmon  from  the  Bishop  of  Aberdeen,  and  a 
few  other  commodities  from  other  correspondents,  the  staple  of  his  exports 
from  Scotland  were  wool  and  hides.  Ainong  many  points  of  great  interest, 
this  merchanfs  book  brings  us  acquainted  with  the  quality  and  price  of 
those  articles  as  early  as  1490 ;  and  at  that  time  the  wool  of  our  Abbey  not 
only  ranked  highest  in  price,  but  seenis  to  have  givcn  a  name  for  the 
highest  quality  of  Scotch  wool.^

The  burning  of  such  a  pile  of  masonry  was  perhaps  but  a  partlal  dcstruc- 
tion.  Certain  it  is,  that  in  a  few  years  after  theEnglishLieutenanfsrough 
handUng,  the  Abbey  buildings  were  sufficiently  restored  to  be  thought  a  con- 
venient  place  for  the  reception  of  a  great  assembly,  since  the  Queen  dowager, 
in  person,  held  there  a  convention  of  tiie  Lords  of  her  party,  preparatory  to 
declaring  war  with  England,  in  1557.  The  subsequent  disappearance  of 
the  ancient  Abbey  buildings  cannot  be  accounted  for  in  the  common  way, 
hv  alleging  the  violence  of  the  reforraing  mobs.  The  Abbot  of  Neubotle 
entered  so  heartily  into  the  lleformation,  that  his  dweUing  must  probably 
have  been  respected  by  the  most  zealous  iconoclasts ;  and  as  it  seems  to 
have  been  a  dweUing  for  his  descendants  continuously,  we  must  rather  seek 
the  cause  in  their  preference  of  modern  comforts  to  thc  picturesque  archi- 
tecture  and  the  historical  and  pious  associations  of  the  old  Abbcy. 

The  present  house  is,  to  outward  appearance,  of  the  style  of  the 
middle  of  last  century,  with  an  addition  made  quite  recently ;  and  iiowever 
much  we  may  wonder  that  the  minister  of  the  parish,  hving  in  the  village,

bactle  Abbey.'"  It  requires  a  close  inspoction  to  correct  this  mistake.  The
